B00BUT5I84|5.0|I can't deny that I've been very excited about this album. Also a little nervous. I am biased as I review it, I can't even imagine what this music might be like to a casual listener. What do they hear? I hear my life. I hear their lives. I hear a community of talent, art, joy, and pain; members far and wide. I hear drama, storms, epic battles, victorious triumphs, history, growth, development and rainbows.<br /> As I continue with my first listen on this one, I listen, listen hard. Don't forget: important music deserves your Full attention. I am truly moved by what I began to hear. Before I knew it I was going along with the saga I was hearing and loving every second of it. But I began to worry...this music was cinematic (and belongs as part of futuristic epic movie, obviously). This music was very dramatic...does that mean I'm in for it in the coming years? What will I need from this album to get me through the times to come? I feel it will launch forth into the deep with me upon a new journey...<br /> With me since inception, each of the last 3 albums by Thirty Seconds to Mars, holds a special place in my past. Each one coming along with me as I was experiencing extremely tumultuous events that would leave behind massive scars, or most blissful memories.  Upon my first listen I remembered where I was with each one past, the first listen, and then what happened during each albums span of years. The first: a terrible death, a relationship gone wrong, a miscarriage, a break-up, and a moving-on. The second: hospitalization, depression, my premature son, and then my joyous first years as a mother. The third: another tragic loss, a terrible fear, a long bed-rest, my daughter who struggled to survive, and then our victorious triumph, deep emotional scars resulting, but 4 beautiful lives striving for our dreams. I got a tattoo for that one.<br /> But on to the music now: Be prepared oh casual-listener-looking-for-new-tunes, this one is not to be taken lightly. I'm not a music expert, just your average audiophile. I find the multi-layered, insanely complex instrumentals, and vocals without comparison, to be some of the most satisfying sounds, and something that shoots straight to my heart and core. Don't be timid - join along with the raw, personal, touching, soul-bearing odyssey. I go on the ups of the powerful, joyous and triumphant waves that are in such songs as Up in the Air and Conquistador. I go on the downs, but there are a lot less of those this album. The head space this band was in, the story they are telling, is full of hope and the beauty of life and pleasure; and how you must fight tooth and nail to get there. Thirty Seconds to Mars always move in the direction our kind is headed, looking far out, and trying to open our eyes to what's happening around us.<br /> If you are a dedicated echelon and are feeling that all too common story of: this isn't my band, this is too different for me and I have to walk away. Well then, I guess you can't be apart of our art-loving forward-thinking family. Go sit in your old comfortable familiar stinky chair and listen to some pop country or some sad emo. But if you can actually hear the music and feel what they feel, and let it connect to your own life, then you might just be ready for this album.<br /> I can't wait to partake in the hive mind that is the concert experience. The joining of voices and body and souls that occurs at all of their live shows. I will be there, at every one I can manage.<br />Now: Jared, Shannon, Tomo: next move - you should make movie scores, these albums are too big to fit inside just a CD.

Through over-processing and 'glitch-work' mainly; but even the majority of vocals not falling into those 2 categories, are just unbearable, considering the source (Jared) & what we all know he's capable of.<br />-- He's got an amazing voice & he's not shy about singing his heart out... and I love the bombastic singing he does; but here, all I hear is a disjointed vocalist who is way too road-weary. Perhaps a longer, well-deserved rest was in order, after their Guinness Record-setting tour.)<br /><br />4) (This is a 2-parter)  Some may find this nit-picky; but this album tells me that Jared (or all of them) think the fans are a bunch of brainless idiots. Plus, there's the actual lyrics, all ofwhich (if listened to even casually) the listener can glean which of the 4 each song is about.  In other words, each song makes it very clear what it's about.... Which ironically, was done for the exact opposite effect... Nevertheless, some of the songs would be better served in a non-grouped tracklisting, as each song tells part of a story/narrative, which was disregarded in favor of 'grouping' them together based on theme. (And yes, anyone can rearrange the tracklist on their own; but that's not the point I'm making here... Just commenting on the ironic, erratic feel it helped perpetuate throughout the album.)<br /><br />All in all, I'd say they went off the deep end while trying to make an album that sounds as if it were produced by Kanye West (who was featured on their last album's, \\"Hurricane 2.0\\").<br />Or, maybe their target audience has changed from teens & young adults, to pre-teens and tweeners, as evidenced by the over abundance of K.West/Lady-Gaga'esque music-style influences in this album.<br />-- Whatever the reason or cause... All I can judge is the end result... And this is (to me) a hugely disappointing album.<br /><br />For the record, so you know more of where I'm coming from, I would rate their albums as:<br />Self-Title Debut = 10/10  (a masterpiece, musically, lyrically, vocally, etc. - A rare gem from start to finish, with 1 exception.)<br />A Beautiful Lie = 7/10  (experimented & went a little too 'punk' on some of it for my taste; but otherwise very solid)<br />This Is War = 9/10  (started their experimentation w/ \\"Electronica\\" genre/style; and the end result was good)<br />L.L.F.+D. = 4/10  (way overboard w/ prior T.I.W. 'experimentation'... lacks quality, substance, and heart/emotion of past albums)<br />-- And honestly, my favorable bias for this band is the only reason I generously gave this 4/10.<br />-- If I were a new-comer, with no knowledge of their past works... B00CRMWMZ0|5.0|Since I first listened to Musique on a compilation disc in 1996 I've been a fan of their unique electronic sound.  There was just something about it, somewhat intangible, but clean and clear.  As they produced and released more music I found my musical taste evolving with them.  I wanted to like them almost as much as I did like them, if that makes sense.<br /><br />With the release of D.A.F.T. I became even more of a fan. The interesting and unique videos really complemented their sound and rounded them out.  Each new release really seemed to propel them, Discovery and its Pop appeal and of course Interstella 5555 was a unique pairing and it amazing how the music and story worked together.  Electroma and Human After All reinforced the humanity of their computer personas, all the while pushing the limits of their unique style while holding on to that intangible unmistakable sound.<br /><br />The Tron Legacy soundtrack and movie cameo was a high point for me, being a huge fan of the original Tron from the 80s and loving all the nostalgia of that time, something about the selection of Daft Punk for the sound track felt like two very significant threads being woven together.  Like something that was meant to be from inception but took time and commitment to get there.<br /><br />Random Access Memories similarly seems to be sewing together the evolution of electronic sound with its roots in Disco, this time kicking the clock back to the 70s.  Is it a formula?  Is Daft Punk weaving itself into our memories like a duo of time traveling robots rewriting history?  It certainly seems like it.<br /><br />I've listened to RAMs straight through 4 times so far and like it better each time, as was always the case with their releases.  I appreciate something new in every pass.  They again have broken new ground, in this case taking us back, but also still have their characteristic daft-hooks worked in here and there to remind you of their roots.<br /><br />I haven't touched on howDaft Punk has influenced the musical landscape with it can clearly be seen in the work of other artists for example Justice, Skrillex hell even Madonna.  I hope by taking us back to the 70s they can trend music in general back.  Disco was highly stigmatized and then abandoned, and despite how it lives on at the heart of House Music, I feel like there is something there that was left on the table.<br /><br />I think Daft Punk has helped to pick it back up, take us back to those days, all while heading toward our man-machine destiny.  In the fewest words, &#34;Digital Nostalgia&#34;.  The theme as I see it is this: as we push technological limits and get closer to becoming one with computers, Daft Punk reminds us to appreciate our organic origins and their artistic creations and our wonderful intangible humanity.<br /><br />I find it interesting that it took 2 pioneering robots-humans to show us this light, though it makes perfect sense.  Computers can access old memories as quickly as recent ones.  In fact they can replay and relive those memories feeling more like time travel than recollection.  I think that is the message of Random Access Memories.  Through the enlightenment of crossing over we will all better appreciate the past...<br /><br />Thank you Thomas and Guy-Manuel!  Digital Pioneers.

159463176X|5.0|Summary: Pari and Abdullah are as close as siblings can be. Abdullah loves his sister so much, she is almost his daughter. So when the powers that be tear them apart, both of their lives are irrevocably changed. Years down the road we meet friends, doctors, chauffeurs, people whose lives are changed through this one event. What unfolds is a story that crosses oceans, teaching us what it means to love and care.<br /><br />Review: Another hit from Khaled Hosseini! Truly, this man knows how to weave a story. And the Mountains Echoed is a less brutal novel than Kite Runner or A Thousand Splendid Suns, but the story is no less meaningful nor less heartbreaking. Each chapter is written from the POV from a different character, and one chapter actually has two different POVs intertwined. Each POV is a different character's journey, but they are all related to each other.<br /><br />Unlike the other two novels, this book does not center around regional conflicts. Hosseini delicately tells us stories of individuals who are going through real life journeys. They love, they die. They acquire dream jobs, and real life illnesses. The story is about individuals and how they relate to one another. Sure, the Taliban and the Russians are mentioned but only as a part of the setting. And since the story takes place in four different countries, we see a vast array of settings that help give more richness to the story.<br /><br />One of the major themes of this novel is appearance vs. reality. The beautiful are empty, the morally repugnant are heroes. What may seem like a good idea at the time is actually the worst idea. The characters are also morally ambiguous, much like the main character in Kite Runner. One of the strength's of Hosseini is he sneaks characters into our hearts so that we may begin to understand their bad and ugly choices. You cannot hate the characters because they are only human.<br /><br />Hosseini does a fantastic job creating unique voices for each of the POVs. Dare I say, even better than GRRM?  The storytelling style even changes, from how a father tells his son a bedtime story, to how a woman gives an interview. It's quite remarkable.<br /><br />One little issue I had was Idris' chapter. Although touching, it had little to do with the larger story. Even Markos' chapter fit. But Idris' chapter wasn't quite like the rest; it fit lyrically, but not contextually. I cannot say more without giving the plot away.<br /><br />We already know Hosseini knows how to tell a story. B0091JQH2Q|5.0|I had these recordings years ago on LPs. Tragically, I sold my LP collection in the 90's as I thought everything was going to be released on CDs....and have never forgiven myself for that move...<br /><br />Over the years, Paavo Berglund revisited these works twice more, once with the Helsinki Philharmonic (also in an EMI box set that contains these recordings of the shorter works here in this set, Bournemouth Symphony) and in his late years with the Chamber Orchestra of Europe. This, to me, was (and is) very interesting, to watch the differences brought to these works through a lifetime of interpretation and rethinking by Berglund. We seldom get a life-long tracery of an artist's thoughts like this in Complete Editions.<br /><br />I am a fan of all three traversals and for a completest like myself give them all kudos for their place. Rightly, though, this first adventuring by Berglund, just as he was truly hitting his stride and reaching great respect in the music world, comes in at first place for the freshness and daring he dislayed here in these pioneering recordings made in the 1970's with the Bournemouth Symphony. Greater thought would come later with the Helsinki, and deep reflection brought to his last set with the COE, but pride of place belongs here in this set.<br /><br />Waiting for what seemed forever for EMI to bring these recordings out, I finally \\"broke down\\" last summer and bought a 2nd hand set from a vendor in Europe of the release on Royal Classics (licensed from EMI) [[ASIN:B00000IGLH Sibelius: Complete Symphonies [Symphony 1-7]]] for over $100 and thought them not well mastered as I seemed to have remembered them from my LP's. Wouldn't you know it, just afterward, here comes EMI with their own Spectacularly Remastered Issue! After a couple conversations about this remastering by EMI with other folks here in the Classical music forums, I purchased this \\"duplication\\"....what a spectacular difference! Each work gains in sonic elevation that is remarkable from that previous released set, but particularly 1, 2, 5, and 7 really stand out through EMI's engineering gurus' efforts. If you want to see what jaw-dropping work can be done with 40+ year old analog recordings, this is definitely a great example! Strings are now opened up, no longer sounding 2-dimensional, brass has gained proper clear and toothy bite and increased depth, and basses are now clearly (and happily) improved dimensionally and no longer sounding \\"blanketed\\"--a revelation from the previous edition I mention above. The orchestra here, now, is represented with proper spaciality. Spectacular for sure, you will not believe these recordings were recorded in the 1960's and 1970's--they rival much more recent recordings and you would swear they are DDD era sessions.<br /><br />These tremendous old recordings have been discussed (here and elsewhere) deeply over the years, and have earned the great praise heaped upon them...my repeating of these thoughts again here is redundant, and so I simply will urge you to add this stand-out issue straightaway to your collection! The price certainly makes this box truly a GIFT to us from EMI!<br /><br />~operabruinarkable from that previous released set, but particularly 1, 2, 5, and 7 really stand out through EMI's engineering gurus' efforts. B008220D5K|4.0|Note: May contain spoilers. Proceed with caution.<br /><br />Six years ago audiences everywhere were presented with Knocked Up. A film many probably went into expecting a raunchy comedy, but one that turned into a charming love story. Well now we're given This Is 40. The \\"sort-of sequel\\" that follows returning characters Pete (Paul Rudd) and Debbie (Leslie Mann) as they cope with their upcoming 40th birthdays. To be completely honest I did find the overall experience to be a bit underwhelming, but I think most of that had to do with the fact that I can't really relate to their situation given my age.<br /><br />The film starts off with Debbie's birthday. She's having a hard time accepting her new age as the viewer sees her constantly deny the fact that she's 40. Normally Pete and Debbie throw a joint birthday party because both of their birthdays are close together, but Debbie says that she doesn't want a party for her and that it should just be for Pete. Well Pete, being a normal guy, doesn't really understand why she's making such a big deal about the whole thing, but he doesn't push her.<br /><br />While Debbie is having her little midlife crisis Pete is having his own personal issues as well. Pete owns a record company that is currently having a difficult time pushing big numbers, his most recent release only sold 600 copies. Pete keeps signing bands that he loves instead of trying to get artists that are more popular within the general public. Because of this he's having huge financial troubles and to top it off he has to lend money to his father because he's retired and recently had triplets. Mind you, Debbie doesn't know that business is bad and she thinks he stopped lending money to his father months ago. Add that to their two daughters (8 & 13) and you have two normal people with a very, very hectic life.<br /><br />That's where I think this film lost me and where it will probably lose many people. This Is 40 is a pretty raw tale of the difficulties that wemust face as we get older. Almost nothing goes according to plan and sometimes we really don't know how to explain to our significant other that we're having a hard time. This isn't necessarily a bad topic to address in a film, but most people who see this are probably in their 20's or in their late 30's/early 40's. The former group doesn't truly understand just how difficult it really is while the latter group doesn't want to be reminded of their own, probably similar, problems.<br /><br />Most of the laughs that can be found throughout This Is 40 aren't necessarily \\"laugh out loud\\" material because of the content, but you're laughing because it's relatable. Whether you're the older audience understanding the little quarrels that are had between Debbie and Pete or the younger audience who understands the rocky relationship between the younger and older siblings, it's bound to make you think of your own experiences and that's where This Is 40 is at its best. When it makes the viewer think back and go, \\"Yea I remember when I had a fight with my older sister.\\" or \\"Yea, I remember when I had a similar argument with my husband/boyfriend.\\" It's these moments where you can find that middle ground that's relatable to majority of the audience.<br /><br />It's no surprise that I enjoyed the casting in this film. Most of the characters in here are returning with their respective actors. I already mentioned Paul Rudd and Leslie Mann, but Jason Segel also returns as Jason, Charlyne Yi returns as Jodi, Tim Bagley returns as Dr. Pellegrino, and Maude and Iris Apatow return as Sadie and Charlotte, respectively. I think it would have been nice to see Seth Rogen and Katherine Heigl make an appearance, but sadly they were nowhere to be found. In terms of new characters, Melissa McCarthy, Chris O'Dowd, Albert Brooks, and John Lithgow were all nice additions. It should be noted that none of these performances are ground breaking. They accomplish what they set out to do and in a movielike this that's all that matters.<br /><br />Overall Score: 7/10 - As I stated earlier I think most of my disappointment was due to my age. I think the core audience is those who are 40+ that can have an easier time relating to these characters and their struggles. Still, given the fact that I've seen Knocked Up multiple times it was nice to see a lot of the same characters again and that alone made the experience worthwhile. If you're even the least bit interested in checking this out then I would say give it a shot. It has a lot of charm and it's bound to make you reminisce regardless of your age.

B009NY43NY|4.0|Well, that's it... it's over. *sigh* I really loved this series and I'm kinda sad to say goodbye to everyone. Of course, I can still catch up with them on HBO via TrueBlood, but it's not the same. Which brings me to a very valid point, it's not the same because the characters aren't the same. Sure, they have the same names and a lot of the same characteristics, but they aren't the same. I like them both in different ways for what they are, but doing side by side comparisons to them is just not fair. Even though the TV series is based on the books (the key word here is `based') they were essentially created by different people. There's HBO's version and Charlaine Harris' version and other than some obvious similarities they went their separate ways. That became fairly obvious when Lafayette survived beyond the first season. I love the fact that the writers decided that he should stick around. It was a perfect step in turning the TrueBlood world into an alternate world inching away from the books. That's just my opinion, so take it for what it's worth, but I do think that the fact that I kept the two separate helped in my acceptance of the course the book series ultimately took. That and the fact that I never really was `team' anyone. I usually don't pick `teams.' I'd rather go with the journey that the author takes me on and enjoy the ride. That's not to say I don't sometimes question decisions that characters make, but I question decisions that people in my real life make all the time. Just because I disagree doesn't make the decision wrong. It just means that I wouldn't have made the same decision they did. (For instance.. I have no doubt that Bella made the right decision when she chose Edward. However, if it were me making the same decision I would have chosen Jacob. Her decision wasn't wrong, it just wasn't the one that I would have made  ) Okay, enough with the soap box...<br /><br />So what did I think of Dead Ever After and do I think it was a fitting end toa series I've followed since the beginning? All in all, I was pretty happy with the story in general. There was mystery, danger, some really happy moments and some really heart breaking ones too. A couple of the characters had to make some really hard decisions and they pretty much stayed true to who they were when they made them. I know that I'm in the minority here, but like I said, it's my opinion. I'm not exactly sure where all the anger is coming from because there were some pretty big clues along the way that pretty much left few choices as to where things were going. I will not be one of those reviewers that gives anything away. Even though I was pretty certain where the story was headed, I was still a little surprised by the decision that Sookie made. Not disappointed though. Not at all.<br /><br />I'm going to miss Bon Temps and the characters that I've grown to love (and sometimes hate) but I think it was time to say goodbye. The great thing about the books though is that I can start the journey all over again if I want. Sure I know what happens, but I always pick up on something new every time I reread a book. It'll be fun to see how many clues I missed along the way.

B005DD7H50|5.0|This film deals with a subject that hasn't often been addressed in films.  What if there was a drug that could enhance your intelligence and give you superior ability?  Would it be worthwhile to take it?  What would be the consequences to your livelihood, your relationships, your personality, your character?<br /><br />All of these issues are explored by the film and in a very realistic way.  The character development, in Eddie's case, is excellent.  Bradley Cooper(Eddie) does an outstanding job of going from a frumpy, down-and-out, aspiriring writer to a slick and competent human being. Then the crash he experiences when he goes through withdrawal from the drug is quite realistic.  I would also say that the script is excellent. I like the way it explores some of the more personal aspects of psychoactive drug use, like \\"Am I really ME when I'm on the drug or am I someone else?\\". The various scenarios (ie. Eddie and his girlfriend, Eddie and a Russian loan shark, Eddie in a day trading firm, Eddie and the corporate big wigs) all come across quite credibly and smoothly.  You know that this is the way that people act and talk.<br /><br />The film does have some plot holes, like<br /><br />1)  The police certainly would have searched Eddie's person and found the money and the stash of pills when they came to<br />  the apartment.  That probably would have ended the story right there.<br /><br />2)  It's doubtful that someone would have framed Eddie for murder just so that they could get the pills.  Why would they think<br />  that it would work?<br /><br />3)  It's not likely that a person as smart as Eddie would not have paid back the loan shark, knowing that his life depended<br />  on it.<br /><br />4)  Why would Eddie even bother going to a loan shark at all?  With his enhanced abilities he could have made millions without that kind of help.<br /><br />These are just a few holes in the story but I'm sure there are others.<br /><br />What concerns me more is how themedication affected Eddie.  I'm concerned that he wasn't more upset about the murdered woman and the role (even unknowingly) he might have played in it.  I'm concerned that he didn't have more compassion for Hank Atwood and didn't try to save his life.  I'm concerned that while vacationing in the Carribean, he comes up with a \\"vision\\" for his life, but his \\"vision\\" basically involves making millions in day trading.  I'm wondering if this is actually part the cautionary tale and that one of the effects of enhanced ability is a lack of concern for others and for the betterment of society.  Does enhanced ability just turn a self-absorbed person into a more successful and adaptable self-absorbed person?  What are the consequences of that?  Maybe that gives some credence to him not paying the loan shark.  Perhaps he was so intoxicated with himself and his new abilities that he neglected to pay.<br /><br />What makes this subject interesting is that a certain class of this type of drug already exists.  They're called amphetamines.  Back in the early twentieth century when amphetamines were first introduced they were considered the wonder drugs of the century.  People who struggled with fatigue, depression, timidity and poor self image took these drugs and experienced an immediate turn-a-round.  They became more productive and successful at work.  They were able to work longer hours without fatigue.  They became more socially engaging and self-assured.  Their family lives became more stable.  But there was a price.  We eventually discovered that these drugs were highly addictive and that users had to keep upping the dose to get the desired effect.  We also discovered that if too much were taken it could cause psychosis.  The wonder drugs turned out to be not so wonderful.<br /><br />I suspect this is the case with most if not all performance enhancing drugs.  Sooner or later, there's going to be a reckoning to pay.  That's why I really didn't like the ending of the film.Eddie's lab perfects the pill to such a degree that he doesn't have to take it any more.  The enhancing effects of the drug are now permanent and his life is now happy ever after.  Oh please!  Wouldn't it be better to have him come down off the meds and learn to cope with his natural imperfections?  I don't know.  I don't know what the ending should be, but I don't think it should be \\"NZT solved all of my problems\\".<br /><br />Unless, of course, there's going to be a Limitless 2.  Hmm.......

His book is a critique of free-market capitalism. He contends that the free-market policies that have dominated in most countries over the last three decades have resulted in slower growth, rising inequality, and heightened instability culminating in the recent economic meltdown. Chang hopes his book will inform the layperson so we all may become active economic citizens. Otherwise, we will always be victims of those in power.<br /><br />Chang addresses each premise separately. He briefly outlines what free-market capitalists tell us and then reveals what we are not told. He writes in a style that is easily understood by those unfamiliar with economic theory. B0000942HV|2.0|Yet another Tubular Bells album! With so many of them out there it's good to know what you are getting before you buy it so I thought I'd try and help out.<br /><br />Tubular Bells 2003 is a note for note re-recording of the 1973 original. In doing so Oldfield utilized nearly all of the original instruments but used modern studio technology. Oldfield was always dis-satisfied with the results first time round complaining he didn't even have time to tune the instruments.<br /><br />So what about the results? Well for me Tubular Bells 2003 sounds far too smooth and clean, the charm and youthful innocence of the original is missing. Sure, the original was a bit rough around the edges but we all loved the character that gave it, it was shot from the hip, not too deliberated, genuine.I was left with the feeling that this was a missed opportunity for a new hand played piece of work that TB2003 hinted Mike could still deliver if he really wanted to.<br /><br />There are two redeeming features for me; Latin is just great, there is a depth that perhaps Mike just couldn't achieve back in '73. Also the appearance of John Cleese as Master of Ceremonies; I always thought there was a touch of Python in Tubular Bells. His announcement of mandolin is inspired! But......Oldfield was obviously so keen on Cleese's delivery he repeats it a few seconds later, utterly pointless and totally irritating.<br /><br />I'm guessing many Oldfield fans will have purchased this release desperate for anything even remotely new from the maestro, my recommendation is to instead invest in the 2010 remastering of the original 1973 Tubular Bells; nothing messed around with, just a much better audio experience. A further recommendation is to find an album called Mohribold by Andrew Taylor. Google it, I downloaded from a site called bandcamp. Mohribold will delight the ears of those who loved Hergest Ridge, Ommadawn and Amarok, it will do me until Oldfield rediscovers his mojo.

1594484805|4.0|As I began to read this very interesting and intriguing book, I realized that I had to forget everything I thought I knew about how to motivate people--at work, at school, or at home. As Daniel Pink explains in this paradigm-shattering text, the secret to high performance and even satisfaction is the human need to direct our own lives, to learn and create new things, and to do better by ourselves and our world. In other words, according to Pink, people are best motivated not by the old, supposedly reliable carrot-and-stick incentives but by \\"intrinsic motivation,\\" wherein the performance of the task is its own reward...when work doesn't feel like work.<br /><br />Based on four decades of scientific research on human motivation, Pink exposes the mismatch between what science knows and what business does. He demonstrates that while the old-fashioned carrot-and-stick approach worked successfully in the 20th century, it's the wrong way to motivate people for today's challenges. In this book, he reveals the three elements of true motivation:<br /><br />*Autonomy--the desire to direct our own lives<br />*Mastery--the urge to get better and better at something that matters<br />*Purpose--the yearning to do what we do in the service of something larger than ourselves<br /><br />All through the book he describes companies that are employing new approaches to motivation and he introduces us to the scientists and entrepreneurs who are taking us down a new road.<br /><br />The third section of the book is a toolkit which has the dual purpose of providing specific ways to implement the principles in the book in a real life setting -- everything from educating your children to managing your exercise schedule.<br /><br />It is difficult to think about a world void of crude incentives. Some may think his assertions are simple or utopian in nature. However, I think this book is full of big ideas that are applicable to the business world, the healthcare world, the educational arena,and even our personal lives. It is a worthwhile read for anyone who enjoys thinking outside of the box and wants to make the world a better place.

B004VMX5TQ|5.0|For the most part I'm not a fan of \\"live\\" CDs but in Bob Seger's case I actually prefer his two live efforts over his studio releases. It often seems that all of the praise is aimed at the excellent [[ASIN:B004VMX5ZK Live Bullet]] to the exclusion of \\"Nine Tonight\\".  However if you're like me then you became familiar with Bob Seger and the Silver Bullet Band through the hits from the trio of blockbuster LPs: [[ASIN:B000038A23 Night Moves]], [[ASIN:B00005OAE6 Stranger in Town]] and [[ASIN:B00009QGDD Against the Wind]]. Those three albums contain the majority of Seger's commercial hits and just about all of them are captured in high energy performances on \\"Nine Tonight\\", Seger's 2nd live set and the one I find myself listening to more often.<br /><br />Again, just about all of the hits are here:<br />From \\"Night Moves\\" - Mainstreet, Rock And Roll Never Forgets, The Fire Down Below, and the title track.<br />From \\"Stranger In Town\\" - Hollywood Nights, Old Time Rock & Roll, We've Got Tonight, and Feel Like a Number.<br />From \\"Against the Wind\\" - Her Strut, You'll Accomp'ny Me, Fire Lake, Betty Lou's Gettin' Out Tonight, and the title track.<br /><br />The only hit from this period missing is \\"Still the Same\\". (Obviously later hits like \\"Like a Rock\\" or \\"Shame on the Moon\\" are not included.) As others have mentioned this live CD actually does a better job of collecting Seger's big hits than the single-disc Greatest Hits collection does. And while Seger and the Silver Bullet Band tend to stick closer to the studio versions of the songs here than they did on \\"Live Bullet\\", there is still plenty of energy and drive behind these performances. To me the sound is better than on Live Bullet and this may be your best bet if you want a good introduction that includes the hits.<br /><br />Ideally, Seger is best heard live and you should have this AND \\"Live Bullet\\" in your collection. And don't worry about duplication: only one selection, \\"Let It Rock\\", is performed on both live sets. All of the tracks from \\"Live Bullet\\" predate \\"Night Moves\\" which is the earliest LP from which the material on \\"Nine Tonight\\" is pulled.

B00C7O8MJ8|4.0|I have never had such a bizarre love/hate relationship with any film composer as I do with Michael Giacchino. There are times when I listen to his music and my jaw goes slack because it's so breathtaking... and then there are times when I grit my teeth and go: \\"What on earth is this idiotic racket?\\" I was willing to completely write him off (something I hardly ever am willing to do with ANY composer, simply because I understand the demands of the job) - that is, until his entries for the '09 \\"Star Trek\\" film proved he can write some tremendous themes, and his scores for \\"John Carter\\" and \\"Super 8\\" proved that he found the 'on' switch somewhere back there.<br /><br />The score for the 2009 \\"Star Trek\\" film was a very dichotomous mixture of insanely good themes (\\"Enterprising Young Men,\\" etc.) and his - as I call it - 'exploding circus' action music (aka, \\"Nailing The Kelvin\\") which is very irritating to listen to on its own, and does not capture the 'organic' action music feel of many other composers. Because music is such a huge part of the Star Trek franchise - remember 1982's \\"Star Trek II: The Wrath of Khan\\" was Horner's first big break, and he did wonderful work there, or remember Jerry Goldsmith and Cliff Eidelman's entries - I was somewhat disappointed with the first score, while simultaneously being enthralled with certain elements.  As a Star Trek fan and a film score buff, I was very, very curious to see what Giacchino did with the new score.<br /><br />First things first: the label's plan to do a double-release of the score (first on digital mp3, before the movie opened, and then on CD yesterday) was profoundly irritating. I like to pick up film scores before I see the movies they go to, and this 'forced' me to buy it twice (!!!) in order to listen to it before I saw this movie. Epic fail (although I'm sure they're very happy to get the money twice).<br /><br />Now for an in-depth review of the actual tracks on the CD, to help understand what I liked and what I did not like. Some of it is personal preference, but it should help some choose whether or not to buy the disc for themselves, if they're torn... I try not to give any spoilers for the film away, but I can't guarantee it, so read carefully, please! :)<br /><br />1. \\"Logos / Pranking The Natives\\" - The Grade-A theme from the '09 movie returns, with a less irritating 'tension-buildup' over the \\"Bad Robot\\" Logos than the previous iteration. The \\"Pranking The Natives\\" portion is a fun, fast-tempo roll through the orchestra. (****)<br /><br />2. \\"Spock Drops, Kirk Jumps\\" - This builds off the main theme in the beginning to a strong percussive beat, and then pushes forward into a rather scatterred, non-melodic bit of tempo-keeping music, which then pulls back together only in the final few seconds into something musically fascinating. (***)<br /><br />3. \\"Sub Prime Directive\\" - A hero/victory piece based on the main themes from the '09 film, brilliantly built upon and thoroughly engaging. Also contains the main Star Trek theme by Alexander Courage. (*****)<br /><br />4. \\"London Calling\\" - A delicate start on the piano, with backbuilding strings building tension steadily to a hair-raising crescendo at the end. Some of the best 2 1/2 minutes of music ever written. I can not give this piece enough credit. 10 stars out of five. (*****)<br /><br />5. \\"Meld-Merized\\" - A sad opening, which then builds tension rather ingeniously before backing off to sad music again. Nicely, nicely done! (*****)<br /><br />6. \\"The Kronos Wartet\\" - Begins with strong percussive and banging sounds backed by a chanting chorus. The most idiotic piece of Klingon music ever, ever written. No Jerry Goldsmith (\\"Star Trek: The Motion Picture\\" [1979], \\"Klingon Battle\\"), James Horner (\\"Star Trek III: The Search For Spock\\" [1984], \\"Klingons\\"), or Cliff Eidelman (\\"Star Trek VI: The Undiscovered Country\\") comparisons possible here. This is literally the worst Star Trek music I've heard -- ever. The chorus sounds like a bunch of apes grunting at the beginning. Might as well be listening to Worf's hideous Klingon opera; Jerry Goldsmith is rolling over in his grave... Then the piece fades from the banging, stupid, ape-chanting sounds and turns introspective on the strings for a moderately interesting ending. The ending boosts the rating to a single star from none. (*)<br /><br />7. \\"Brigadoom\\" - Very interesting theme for the Cumberbatch villain. Starts delicately and then builds militaristically to a very interesting conclusion. I wish that the 'buildup' lasted for a little longer, as just when the foot starts tapping to it it ends; still, it's very nice. (****)<br /><br />8. \\"Ship To Ship\\" - High tension piece; fortunately not as 'exploding circus' as some of Giacchino's previous action music (such as his \\"Mission Impossible III\\" lunacy, or \\"Nailing The Kelvin\\" on the '09 Star Trek CD). The ending of the piece is a very nice building crescendo. (***)<br /><br />9. \\"Earthbound And Down\\" - This is an interesting piece that works very well in the film for building the tension; much of it is not very 'musical' when listened to as a standalone piece, being more of a jarring 'tumble through the orchestra', but again we are fortunately spared the infamous 'exploding circus' sound. (***)<br /><br />10. \\"Warp Core Values\\" - A militaristic start on the drums indicating someone's on a 'mission' turns into a hero piece that is very engaging... all in the first minute. This is followed by twenty seconds of 'tension-building' before turning into 'tragic' music that is extremely effective and enjoyable. (*****)<br /><br />11. \\"Buying The Space Farm\\" - A very sad piece which is based in large part on \\"Labor of Love\\" from the previous film (which also happened to be one of the best segments of music in that movie). At 2:43, the music begins to turn completely into something fast-tempo and 'angry' ... perfect for the scene. (****)<br /><br />12. \\"The San Fran Hustle\\" - A largely fast-tempo chase cue, with some slower music interspersed between bursts of kinetic energy. Most of this action music is not very musically interesting when listened to apart from the film on the CD (it actually starts to get annoying on its own about three minutes in), but it does drive the tempo in the actual movie. I prefer a more organic approach to action music, but this is simply a matter of taste. At about 3:24, the piece actually gets very musically interesting, and remains that way until the end, even building off some of the themes from the first film. (***)<br /><br />13. \\"Kirk Enterprises\\" - A piece building nicely off some of the themes from the previous film, but changed up and made 'fresh' again. (****)<br /><br />14. \\"Star Trek Main Theme\\" - Overall, an excellent piece to conclude the score, building on the themes from the '09 film very appropriately. My one complaint (and it's a big one) is that the timing of the notes for the Alexander Courage \\"Star Trek\\" theme (about 3:00 minutes in) is absolutely butchered again, just as Giacchino did at the end of the previous film. I mean - it's tortured. Any Star Trek fan who pays attention to the music (like me) has to be all but tearing his hair out at this insistence on changing it up just to show it was changed. Overall, though, the piece is terrific. (****)<br /><br />15. \\"The Growl\\" (Digital bonus track not included on CD) - A club / dance song. Played in the scene where Scotty is in the bar in San Francisco. A mediocre addition to the score that seems very out of place compared to the orchestral music. (** for 'fit' with the score; *** or **** as a standalone song.)<br /><br />Overall score: 3.7 out of 5.<br /><br />To summarize, I feel that Giacchino did a nice job building off his themes from the 2009 movie, and most of his action music in this film is less jarring than his older 'exploding circus' sounds. This is the case even if it is not always as melodic or musically interesting as other music from the Star Trek universe by the Goldsmith or Horner.

B00B2QG3K2|4.0|I have to admit it, I was a fan of the Farro Brothers.  The two brothers had such a distinctive sound with Josh's shimmering guitar tones and Zac's attacking approach to the drums.  And while that is missed on this album, Haley Williams and Company have plunged ahead with an enjoyable bag of treats, albeit a mixed bag at times.<br /><br />1. Fast In My Car (10/10): Starts out with a cool little guitar riff.  It's a fun song while at the same time it is a proclamation of who they are now as a band and their new outlook.  It's basically a song about moving on and their &#34;drive&#34; to do it.  It's Paramore at the their energetic best.<br /><br />2. Now (10/10): The first single from the new album shows you that Paramore is here to stay.  It has fuzzy guitars, killer vocals, and a mantra of &#34;If there's a future, we want it NOW.&#34;  It is song of rebirth that is full of hope and adrenaline.<br /><br />3. Grow Up (8/10): Very poppy and it makes me wonder if Haley is channeling her inner Gwen Stefani.  Is this a song a response to Eisley's &#34;Sad&#34; that is supposed to be about Haley dating Sherri Dupree's ex, New Found Glory's Chad Gilbert?  Some may think so but I see it as another song in a theme-filled album about maturing and moving on from the past.<br /><br />4. Daydreaming (10/10): As much as I love an energetic Paramore, this slow pop song gets me every time.  I love the music build up and Haley's vocals sound great.  Has a bit of an 80's feel, maybe even a little bit of a Blondie feel to it.<br /><br />5. Interlude: Moving On (7/10): As I mentioned before, there is a theme about maturing and moving on, so it is fitting to have a song called Moving On.  This is one of several interludes with Haley and an ukulele and this is the strongest one.  With lyrics like what follows, it seems clear that it is a song directed at the Farro brothers: &#34;Let 'em play their song, let 'em say what's right and wrong. Let 'em do their thing, cause it'll never be you and me. We got out own style, we got a way of living life. If they can't get down, well then we don't need them around. Well I could be angry, but you're not worth the fight and besides I'm moving on. I've counted to 10 and now I'm feeling all right. And besides I'm moving, yes I'm moving, besides I'm moving on.&#34;<br /><br />6. Ain't It Fun (10/10): An 80's sounding funky dance/pop song, this is another song that sounds different for Paramore and yet it works.  I love the sarcastic chorus of: &#34;Ain't it fun living in the real world?&#34;  A very cool bridge as well.  The highlight of the album.<br /><br />7. Part II (5/10): Part II is in reference to being a newly re-written version of &#34;Let The Flames Begin.&#34;  It is almost as if Paramore is saying we still have our Christian faith intact and that we still have the struggles as all Christians have.  As a fan of the original song, I have to say that I am less of a fan of this version.  It does make you miss the Farro brothers when you hear it.<br /><br />8. Last Hope (8/10): Part II does transition nicely into this song, a song about hope and faith.  I love the lines: &#34;It's just a spark but it's enough to keep me going. And when it's dark out and no one's around it keeps glowing.&#34;  It's another slower song that builds nicely and the guitar tones are reminiscent of what Josh Farro would write.<br /><br />9. Still Into You (9/10): A nice love song.  Catchy and cutesy.  If the guitars were a little heavier, I think I would have enjoyed it a little more.  This is definitely a song that when you listen to it, you realized just how much Zac Farro's drumming is missed.<br /><br />10. Anklebiters (8/10): Paramore picks up the pace and that might be an understatement.  This song is a nice little punk tune about society's attack on people.  Haley is declaring that she is over all these &#34;anklebiters&#34; and is done trying to please everyone.  It goes along with the recurring theme of moving on as a band and as a person.<br /><br />11. Interlude: Holiday (3/10): Another interlude song about moving on and getting a deserved break.  It is short and seems like a filler track (though with 17 tracks total, it is hard to complain).<br /><br />12. Proof (8/10): This song has some cool guitar lines and cool drum beats with a lot of energy.  Seems like a song about long distance relationships which she has a lot of experience in.  About 2/3 of the way into, it gets a nice bouncy feel to the song.<br /><br />13. Hate To See Your Heart Break (9/10): A slower song that uses Haley's lower range.  This has the feel of a country pop song.  I could hear it being used as a country song on the TV show &#34;Nashville&#34; or hear a country artist do this song.  I like the emotion in Haley's voice during the chorus.  A very different sound for Paramore but for a group that maturing and moving on, I think it is great.  There is nothing better than a band that can excel at upbeat songs AND slower songs.<br /><br />14. (One Of Those) Crazy Girls (5/10): I'm not crazy about (One Of Those) Crazy Girls.  It does have an interesting slow start that speeds up, but not one of my favorites.<br /><br />15. Interlude: I'm Not Angry Anymore (3/10): Another song about Josh Farro?  At this point, we could all care less since I thought we were moving on.<br /><br />16. Be Alone (8/10): This is one of the more old-Paramore sounding songs.  Nice sounding drums and guitars and Haley's outstanding vocals. Both catchy and energetic, it comes at just the right time in the album.<br /><br />17. Future (7/10):  &#34;We don't talk about the past; we don't talk about the past now.&#34;  Well, I don't know if that is true but this is a decent ending to an above average album.  The song reminds of the way Anberlin likes to end albums except I wish Paramore had built it into this rocking, epic finale.  Even though the music ends and then they come back in, I feel like they could have and should have gone out a little bigger.<br /><br />In conclusion, I enjoyed this mixed bag of treats.  Sure, there were some lows but there were a lot of high moments.  One thing I did love is although I have been such a fan of Paramore's upbeat/energetic songs, there were some slower songs here that really stuck with me.  And whatever your opinion of Haley, you have to admit she has one of the best vocals out there and proves that she is not limited to a certain genre.  As far as her and the Farro brothers are concerned, I believe there are three sides to every story: hers, theirs, and the truth somewhere in the middle.  We can wonder why Haley was signed before the rest of the band, but at the end of day, Haley chosen then and NOW that she longs to be in a band and not a solo artist.  And I am grateful for that choice.g on and getting a deserved break.  It is short and seems like a filler track (though with 17 tracks total, it is hard to complain).<br /><br />12. B00CTSDDVO|4.0|I'll say up front: There are spoilers in this review. If you haven't seen the movie yet and plan to, don't read any further.<br /><br />Those who don't mind extra background info before they go to the theater, read on:<br /><br />\\"Star Trek: Into Darkness\\" is not a perfect film. But for pure viewing enjoyment it's still, in my opinion, the third-best of the 12 films in the series, up there with \\"Star Trek 4: The Voyage Home\\" and \\"Star Trek 2: The Wrath of Khan.\\" Given the spotty track record of the series overall, you can take that comment as you will.<br /><br />What's good:<br />* The first 20 minutes are among the best opening of any ST film.<br />* The interaction among the ensemble clearly carries the film and keeps it from bogging down. It's obvious that this cast really gets along and has looked forward to doing another movie together.<br />* Benedict Cumberbatch's villain, \\"John Harrison\\" (a.k.a. the legendary \\"Khan\\"), is a perfect foil for Kirk -- and later, Spock. Peter Weller's Admiral Marcus gets a good runner-up award.<br />* Zoe Saldana has become THE Uhura. Nichelle Nichols must be asking herself, \\"What if...?\\"<br />* There's a good interweaving of the original Star Trek series episode \\"Space Seed,\\" and \\"Star Trek 2: The Wrath of Khan,\\" with a touch of \\"Star Trek 6: The Undiscovered Country,\\" and the 1986 Diane Carey ST novel \\"Dreadnought!\\"<br />* Various references to events and characters from the original series, sprinkled throughout the film in an almost \\"Where's Waldo?\\" motion for fans.<br />* Seeing Kirk being called on the carpet by Admiral Pike for his recklessness, and watching him grow up on screen as events unfold. Nice work, Chris Pine.<br /><br />What's not so good:<br />* Timeline continuity issues, such as the crew's journey to the Klingon world of Kronos, where you see a partially destroyed moon orbiting the planet. The accident that caused this destruction took place in Star Trek 6, about 25 years after the \\"Space Seed\\" episode. Or the references to the tribbles and the \\"Mudd incident,\\" which took place in the series a year or two after this film's present time.<br />* Believability: The Enterprise underwater like a giant sub? Spock in an all-out brawl with Khan, and emerging without a scratch (Kirk, on the other hand, has quite a few scrapes he carries through the entire film)? A space station orbiting Jupiter that no one sees? And no one, other than the original Spock, has any memory or knowledge of who Khan is/was?<br />* Numerous plot holes, some big enough to drive a starship through. Among the biggest offenders: handheld communicators that can call from the Neutral Zone all the way to Earth (a feat not even possible in the \\"Next Generation\\" series about 80 years later), but can't reach the orbiting Enterprise during a volcanic explosion; Dr. McCoy's statement about needing Khan's blood to save Kirk's life, when he has 72 other persons with the same blood in his medical lab; the same Dr. McCoy saying that he can't remove someone from a cryotube without killing him, but later taking someone out of one of those tubes and telling his assistants to just \\"keep him in a medically induced coma\\"; Khan getting stunned once with a phaser and going down, unconscious, but later getting hit several times point blank and he's not even dazed; Carol Marcus has a British accent, while dad Admiral Marcus doesn't; a tribble sitting on Dr. McCoy's desk, that somehow doesn't move despite the fact the Enterprise has been tossed around like a rag doll; a security officer who doesn't know what a communicator is, or what a countdown is (\\"10, 9, 8...\\")...I could go on, but you get the idea.<br />* The Federation, thanks to the original Mr. Spock in the last movie, has the technology for long-distance teleportation. Yet the Enterprise isn't using it; but the villains are.<br />* The Enterprise has obviously had an upgrade: In some respects, it's more technologically advanced than the Enterprise of the Next Generationseries and movies.<br /><br />Judgment call:<br />* Replaying the ST2 death scene between Kirk and Spock, with the roles reversed: Kirk dying in the radiation chamber after saving the ship; Spock on the outside, in anguish. I thought it was a fine homage to the original; others may consider the word-for-word scene remake is merely lazy scriptwriting.<br />* Reviving Kirk about 20 minutes after his death. This is a universe with an alternate timeline. Imagine what could have been from this point with Spock (or someone else) permanently in the captain's chair?<br />* The \\"gratuitous\\" scenes of Dr. Marcus and Captain Kirk in their underwear (at different times), which do nothing to forward the plot (but obviously kept certain people interested).<br /><br />Overall: A worthwhile film, if you look past the flaws. You just wonder what they're going to do now that the crew is officially on its \\"five year mission to go where no one has gone before.\\" Maybe...the Mirror Universe?

Instead of a story involving fantastic and futuristic inventions the lead (and nearly sole) character in The Martian uses extant technology and current science to solve his problems. Instead of a novel with an extremely capable protagonist who never makes mistakes and succeeds with every effort we're given a very human character with whom we all can empathize and identify.<br /><br />All in all, it's a pleasantly and surprisingly good novel. Bonus: Drummer Nick Mason's distorted voice yelling &#34;One of these days I'm going to cut you up into little pieces!&#34;<br /><br />&#34;A Pillow of Winds&#34; is one of the relatively few love songs Pink Floyd ever wrote, but its soothing melody and lyrics about going to sleep calm the listener. Ringing acoustic and slide guitars keep the track interesting, and the slide adds a slightly disconcerting bluesy feel.<br /><br />&#34;Fearless&#34; is a slow soft-rock song, again with ringing guitars. Bonus: A recording of a crowd singing Rodgers' and Hammerstein's &#34;You'll Never Walk Alone&#34; is played in the background, and as the &#34;Fearless&#34; theme fades out the chanting voices are left, providing an uplifting finale to the song.<br /><br />&#34;San Tropez&#34; and &#34;Seamus&#34; close side one of the album. They are weaker than the other three tracks on side one, but they are certainly not bad. &#34;Tropez&#34; is the only song sung by Roger Waters in the album. It is jazzy, with a piano solo by keyboard player Richard Wright. &#34;Seamus&#34; is a slow acoustic blues, which features a dog barking in the background along with the music. The dog, Seamus (the song was named after him), belonged to Steve Marriott of the Small Faces and Humble Pie.<br /><br />And finally, &#34;Echoes.&#34; &#34;Echoes,&#34; at over 23 minutes in length, provides the astonishing climax to the album. The verses are sung in a haunting duet by David Gilmour and Richard Wright. After the main verses, a slow, fuzzy guitar solo by Gilmour builds up to a funky section which features Waters and Wright playing chunky fills on each of their instruments, while a mournful guitar wails over the top. This section fades out, and the &#34;seagull section&#34; begins (the distinctive screeching sound is Gilmour playing his guitar through a wah-wah pedal and probably a slide), after which a section with just guitar and organ begins. After this, more verses are sung, and the rest of the track progresses similarly to the first seven minutes. A white noise concludes the song and the album.<br /><br />After this album, Pink Floyd finally found their spot in rock history after confused albums such as &#34;Ummagumma&#34; and &#34;Atom Heart Mother.&#34; Soon afterward, they stopped writing film scores, and concentrated all of their energy on the landmark albums yet to come.Richard Wright. B009NYF2ES|5.0|Appearing in various forms from short stories (1930's) to straight play (1950's) to musical play (1960's) to the darkest of musical films, &#34;Cabaret&#34;, the 1972 recipient of 8 Academy Awards including Best Actress, Best Supporting Actor and Best Director ( the Best Picture and Adapted Best Screenplay Oscars went to &#34;The Godfather&#34;) , has now arrived, along with an extremely informative and appreciative book, in a stunning Blu-ray upgrade from Warner Brothers. Frankly, the film hasn't looked and sounded this good since its original big screen incarnation. If any musical of the past 40 years deserves such a deluxe treatment, it is &#34;Cabaret&#34;.<br />Giving the performances of their lives, both Liza Minnelli, as the self-deluded but highly ambitious anti-heroine Sally Bowles, and Joel Grey, as the Mephistophelean M.C., transport us to the tawdry ambience of the 1931 Weimar Republic, where sexual identity is fluid and everything and everyone is for sale (the contemporary club scene, anyone?). The &#34;Money Money&#34; number about the erotic attraction of wealth could be successfully placed into the soundtrack of &#34;The Great Gatsby&#34;(2013).<br />Under the sure and certain control of choreographer/director Bob Fosse, &#34;Cabaret&#34; the film solves the traditional problem of the musical play and the musical film. Since every number, save one, in the surprisingly contemporary score takes place in the seedy Kit Kat Klub in Berlin, where corpulent businessmen mingle with uniformed Nazi thugs and prostitutes, the element of realism can be planted and nurtured. Characters don't suddenly erupt in song. Every Kit Kat number comments on what is going on in the alleys, on the streets and in the boardinghouses: the brutal tactics of the surging National Socialist Party, the increasingly lethal rise of anti-Semitism (also reflected in the doomed love story between the secondary characters Natasha and Fritz), the nostalgia for the old order which Sally's landlady harbors.<br />&#34;Tomorrow Belongs to Me&#34;, the sole number set outside amidst the natural beauty of the German countryside and the Gemutlichkeit of the German biergarten, is also the perfect combination of music and political commentary. As a scout-uniformed, perfect Aryan boy begins to sing what will become a Nazi anthem embraced by a multi-class, multi-generational audience, the camera slowly pans down from his angelic face to the swastika prominently displayed on his uniform. When Brian and Max , bisexual lovers who form a sexual triangle with Sally, leave the scene in a limousine, Fosse inserts a knowingly sardonic shot of the smiling M.C.<br />The film opens and closes with distorted funhouse mirror images of the audience at the Kit Kat Klub, slowly melding from black and white to color at the beginning and finishing with shots of a predominately Nazi  audience. When a mirror was used onstage in the musical play, the audience could see itself. Geoffrey Unsworth's Oscar winning cinematography, rendered here with solid blacks and the subtly shifting quality of stage lighting, puts us there. Though the Weimar era was 80 years ago, (think Dietrich in &#34;The Blue Angel&#34; (1931), Fosse, Minnelli and Grey took us there 40 years ago and continue to do so to this day. The hypnotic spell of &#34;Cabaret&#34; lives and breathes on this splendid disc.

B009IEXKXI|4.0|No little green men here. This is an American astronaut stranded on Mars when his crew inadvertently leave him behind. It's not as stupid a scenario as it sounds, but what ensues is an epic survival story of how the hero exists on what little has been left behind by the Mars mission. No palm trees or fish laden seas for this castaway, just barren rock and dust. The book is basically his log, which is written in a folksy and irreverent style, with great humour and pathos. There is a fair bit of techno-speak to give the book authenticity and it really is quite believable. You start to identify with him and root for him to survive. Meanwhile in Houston Texas, Mission Control has realised with a mixture of horror and joy that there is a survivor, one albeit balanced precariously between survival and untimely demise. They set about to try to bring him home and this naturally becomes the most watched event in history world-wide.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed the book. It has lots of dark humour, suspense and enough of a story to keep me turning the pages. The interaction between the Martian and Houston is very funny at times and has the kind of dialogue which you could imagine happening if this was a real life scenario. His journal relates his day to day activities, some of them routine but many of them ground breaking as he adapts to his predicament with stoic humour and extraordinary ingenuity. There are plenty of setbacks along the way. Most of us would have given up and taken the red pill long before the end.<br /><br />I'd recommend this book highly to anyone who wants something completely different from the routine survivor novel. It is really quite ingenious. I wish I had written it. The plot and story line have real credibility which makes the book much more than a sci-fi novel. In the end you have to love the guy. The actual book ending is a bit of an anti-climax, well it was for me anyway, but long before that, I was fully engaged and can forgive the author anything. I am glad I read it and I am sure I will read it again. I hope they make a movie out of it to be honest.

B00BY8DC2A|5.0|If something can be said for the Brand New Heavies its that personally I feel their funk style has grown,expanded and developed in time. While their first couple of albums in the early 80's had their moments,they were honestly a bit overrated as full on concepts. Some may debate that. Still the bands ambitions to introduce the musically collective live band spirit of funk/soul/dance music to a generation saturated with pre-programmed New Jack Swing. With such a noble musical statement of intent,how could any artist fully live up to it at such an early stage? Luckily time has been kind to them. Even the more pre-programmed music of today tends to veer in rhythmic intent to what the Acid Jazz era funk revivalists like BNH did in the early 90's. So an appreciation for what they do is very much in the air at this particular musical moment. So after seven years in the wilderness, Brand New Heavies have returned. And interestingly enough so has N'Dea Davenport.<br /><br />  Now the opening title song,\\"Sunlight\\",\\"On The One\\",N'Dea's marvelous performance on \\"Addicted\\" as well as \\"Turn The Music Up\\" and \\"Heaven\\" are all high octane, highly crafted horn packed bass/guitar oriented late 70's type funk-very much the love child of Earth Wind & Fire and Chic,meaning an explosively powerful combination with their own electric piano based jazzier spirit too.  \\"Do You Remember\\" is however my personal favorite here. Its in that same vein. But something about the way the melodies,bass lines and keyboards all overlap in this churning,pulsing harmonies hits exactly the right spot for this approach to the funk groove. The instrumental \\"Itzine\\",\\"The Way It Goes\\" and \\"Lights\\" go more for the high stepping,foot stomping end of funk. Still there's still that sleekness there with a strong Crusaders type vibe which is much appreciated. \\"Spice Of Life\\" is a well played attempt perhaps at fusing electronica based acid jazz with dub/reggae,but it is not my personal favorite here and probably not the strongest number on the album. \\"One More For The Road\\" ends the album with another strong funk groove-fusing the more high stepping with the high crafted.<br /><br />  There's an overall impression here that the Brand New Heavies are not going to be away quite so long this time around. In all honesty I haven't consistently followed up with every single one of their albums-especially those released after the new millennium rang in. For one,a couple of them were never released stateside. From what I have heard,there were attempts to re-up with new lead singers and on at least one occasion an attempt at a more guitar rock oriented type of sound. At the same time they kept returning to their roots,kept returning to the funk. And it can't be stated enough that they not only looked to improve their own take on the funk,but the progression of the genre as well. It is this sort of album that creates the overall impression that a strong funk revival of the type that originally bought the Brand New Heavies into existence might just be ascendant within this decade. This is a musical generation that needs to understand what funk is about instrumentally,the musical challenges and resulting joys it brings and provides. Compared to some of the very beige colored pop of that era and beyond,this type of new music had heart. It had a musical and rhythmic truth. And it was as much the funk as one could live! For a DJ duo whose sound of the time was built on densely layered samples I got this impression the entire \\"french house\\" movement served a similar function as Acid Jazz had before it: disguised terms for funk music. Of course Daft Punk made it clear on their first album that people such as George Clinton were significant inspirations to their musical vision. Of course any DJ/electronica artist could easily embrace Bernie Worrell's \\"video game\\" synthesizer sound and have. Daft Punk understand more. They understand the song craft, subtleties of harmony and thrill of innovation-getting their listeners to stretch their ears. And most of all they understand rhythm. After producing live and soundtrack work and remixes Daft Punk are back with a full on studio album. And the all the way live instrumental style of funk has finally arrived in their musical lives!<br /><br />  \\"Give Life Back To Music\\" is a creamy dance/funk number,right out of the bass/guitar mode of Chic of course yet done so well and not only that but puts a strong manifesto,even just by the title alone,to the function of funkiness in music as a positive force. \\"The Game Of Love\\",Within\\"-with its beautiful piano intro and \\"Beyond\\" really fascinate me because the vocals are still handled with genuine vocorders-NOT autotune. The album end with \\"Contact\\",a loose ended combination of 70's funk with 90's drum n bass effects. I am unsure what electronica/house/DJ or even modern dance music lovers will make of this album. In it's most basic sense it is a full on funk and dance/groove/disco styled album-based fully in live instrumentation or electronics used in an organic fashion. But really that's the very fruit of Daft Punk's entire musical tree. To me,this is likelythe best album they've released so far. B00CAUH7UE|4.0|I'll be honest, I had a hard time getting into this book. But once I did, I was not disappointed that I read it.<br /><br />Give and Take looks at who are the most successful people in the world: givers, takers or matchers. What he found from all walks of life, those that are givers are more successful than takers or simply matchers. This is counter-intuitive and what makes the book so good. This book seeks to answer this question: Every time we interact with another person we have a choice to make: do we try to claim as much value as we can, or contribute value without worrying about what we receive in return?<br /><br />While our culture tends to shun givers at the beginning, in the long run according to Grant, \\"Those who give first are best positioned for success later.\\"<br /><br />So what are givers? They are what Grant calls Otherish: \\"If takers are selfish and failed givers are selfless, successful givers are otherish: they care about benefiting others, but they also have ambitious goals for advancing their own interests. Being otherish means being willing to give more than you receive but still keeping your own interests in sight, using them as a guide for choosing when, where, how, and to whom you give.\\"<br /><br />I particularly found the chapter on why givers burnout very relevant to pastors. Here's what Grant said: \\"Principle of giver burnout: it has less to do with the amount of giving and more with the amount of feedback about the impact of that giving. Givers don't burn out when they devote too much time and energy to giving. They burnout when they're working with people in need but are unable to help effectively. Givers burnout not because they are giving too much but because they don't feel like their giving is making a difference.\\"<br /><br />Here are a few more things that jumped out:<br /><br />-When takers and matchers network, they tend to focus on who can help them in the near future, and this dictates what, where, and how they give. Their actions tend to exploit a common practice in nearly all societies around the world, in which people typically subscribe to a  norm of reciprocity: you scratch my back, I'll scratch yours. If you help me, I'm indebted to you, and I feel obligated to repay.<br />-People are more likely to benefit from weak ties.<br />-You should be willing to do something that will take you five minutes or less for anybody.<br />-Takers have a knack for generating creative ideas and championing them in the face of opposition. Because they have supreme confidence in their own opinions, they feel free of the shackles of social approval that constrict the imaginations of many people.<br />-Here's the definite feature of how givers collaborate: They take on the tasks that are in the group's best interest, not necessarily their own personal interests.<br />-Even when people are well intentioned, they tend to overvalue their own contributions and undervalue those of others.<br />-Success doesn't measure a human being, effort does.<br />-Interest precedes the development of talent. It turns out that motivation is the reason that people develop talent in the first place.<br />-In roles as leaders and mentors, givers resist the temptation to search for talent first. By recognizing that anyone can be a bloomer, givers focus their attention on motivation.<br />-Givers focus on gritty people.<br />-When our audiences are skeptical, the more we try to dominate them, the more they resist.<br />-In teams and service relationships, powerless speech is actually more influential than powerful speech.<br />-Successful givers, it turns out, are just as ambitious as takers and matchers.<br />-Takers tend to care more about benefitting personally form their jobs, givers care deeply about doing jobs that benefit other people.<br />-The perception of impact serves as a buffer against stress, enabling employees to avoid burnout and maintain their motivation and performance.<br />-Research shows that if people start volunteering two hours a week, their happiness and satisfaction, and self-esteem goes up a year later (Pastors, use this the next time you vision cast about serving at your church).<br />-People who initially give things away for self reasons begin to care about the people they're helping.<br />-Common ground is a major influence on giving behaviors (Pastors, use this idea when vision casting about giving and fundraising).<br />-People often take because they don't realize that they're deviating from the norm.<br />-When we try to predict other's reactions, we focus on the cost of saying yes, overlooking the cost of saying no (This has enormous implications on next steps in preaching).<br />-If many people believe in giving, but assume others don't, the whole norm in a group or company can shift away from giving (How a church becomes more giving).<br /><br />I saved this last quote because it has enormous implications on preaching, discipleship and next steps: \\"According to a study led by NYU psychologist Peter Gollwitzer, people who went public with their intentions to engage in an identity relevant behavior were significantly less likely to engage in the behavior than people who kept their intentions private. When people made their identity plans known to others, they were able to claim identity without actually following through on the behavior.\\"<br /><br />I found this book particularly helpful because many of the people who are pastors, work in ministry or volunteer at a church are givers. Grant lays out what characteristics givers have, how to encourage them to reach their potential and not burnout. A helpful book for any pastor.

B002ZRPQ5K|2.0|It's always refreshing to see an artist step out of their comfort zone and try something new. The great ones always do and it's with this ambitious spirit that Keane released this 2010 EP. Being a huge fan of the band's previous three records, I was hopeful that this would continue their streak of mixing gorgeous melodies with subtle experimental flourishes.<br /><br />Then I gave it a listen. Rapping. The Rocky theme. Singing in Japanese. An entire track of ambient noise. I'll give them this: those elements were certainly unexpected. They also feel incredibly forced and self-indulgent, something that you could never accuse the band of being before. Before this each album had shown signs of growth and branching out, yet they always complimented the songs and didn't feel tacked on. Unfortunately they add a level of cheese to Night Train that makes it hard to rally around. \\"My Shadow\\", \\"Stop for a Minute\\", and \\"Clear Skies\\" are all decent and sound much more natural; they're allowed breathing room and come off as taking the best of the band's previous work and melding it together. These are definitely the tracks to start with and should be easy for any listener to adjust to. Everything else is a gamble that you may or may not enjoy.<br /><br />Thankfully the band was able to use this EP to get these ideas out of their system, leaving them free to make Strangeland, which is a brilliant and resonant album that does everything right. I have to give them credit for trying something new but just because it's different doesn't automatically make it good. This step was unnecessary but thankfully there are a few salvageable tracks that save it from being a complete throwaway.

1250012570|3.0|What an intense first prologue. It really captures what the book is like. \\"Set over the course of one school year in 1986, Eleanor and Park is the story of two star-crossed misfits - smart enough to know that first love almost never lasts, but brave and desperate enough to try.\\" Eleanor and Park didn't immediately draw me in; it's a book that starts slowly. You don't realize that you're absorbed until it's too late, just like Eleanor and Park don't realize how much they care for one another until it's too late.<br /><br />That said, contemporary romance is really not one of my favorite genres, and as I was reading Eleanor & Park I remembered why. It's a better romance than most, obviously, but it was still really sappy and unrealistic at times. It was kind of like insta-love. I could really see how Eleanor and Park might go from ignoring one another to being friends, but the romance, the central part of the story, just didn't work for me. How could they suddenly love each other so, so much after like two weeks? It all felt forced, and the construct of them reading comics together didn't feel real to me.<br /><br />I did like the contrast between Eleanor's home life and Park's home life. Park's family is basically perfect, except for some minor quarrels with his father, whereas Eleanor is living in a tiny home with her mother, siblings, and her horrible step-father. But the thing is, even that didn't feel right to me. Nothing about this realistic novel seemed realistic. There are fantasies that feel more realistic than Eleanor & Park, if that makes sense. The world created in some fantasy novels just works, but it didn't in this novel. I also didn't understand why Eleanor's mom put up with her second husband, who's clearly a jerk. The book isn't set in the 19th century; it's set in 1986. There's such a thing as a divorce, as the police.<br /><br />But the thing is, in some ways, I really liked Rainbow Rowell's writing style even though so many aspects of the novel didn't work for me. I think she has major potential, but this one just didn't work for me. But she is a good writer, and another book could be very good. She already has one other novel, Attachments.<br /><br />I guess I was just expecting Eleanor & Park to be very good and very life-changing. It was neither of those.  What it was was full of cliches. The whole love-at-first sight thing can work sometimes, but it didn't work in Eleanor & Park. I got really annoyed with both of the main characters. Also, there seems to be a thing to set YA fiction in the late 80's and early 90's, so that you can have mix-tapes and Walkman's. As far as I can see, that and the music they were listening to was the only reason that Eleanor & Park is set in 1986. So the time period really didn't work for me either.<br /><br />(Sigh). I really wanted to like this book, but I didn't. At times it was even cringe-worthy, and I struggled with finishing it. It was a real disappointment to me, although I will say that it was very gripping and the later sections were a bit better. I also liked the way the book ended, and the second half redeemed itself a bit. For the type of book that it is, Eleanor & Park has a great ending, but overall it left me wanting something more. Thanks to St. Martin's for providing me with a review copy.<br /><br />www.novareviews.blogspot.com
